\chapter{Thematic dictionary} \label{thematic dictionary}

\section{Kinship terms} \label{kinship terms (list)}

Pronominal prefix choice for kinship terms is described in \sectref{ch:Kinship terms and pronominal prefix choice}.
\begin{multicols}{2}


\thematicheadword{adopted child} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{de-\textsc{p}-hsnyeˀ-ǫh} {\dualic}-\textsc{p}-care.for-{\stative}. 
Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{deyago̱hsnyéˀǫh} \trs{her adopted daughter}

\thematicheadword{aunt (maternal)} 
\stem{\textsc{int}-nohá:-ˀah-(shǫ:ˀǫh)} \textsc{int}-aunt-{\diminutive}-({\pluralizer}). 
Different-generation kinship term. This word used to refer only to one’s mother’s sister. Now, it can also refer to one’s father’s sister.
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫkino̱há:ˀah} \trs{our aunt}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gakeno̱haˀahsǫ́:ˀǫh} \trs{my aunts}


\thematicheadword{aunt (maternal)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-nohá:-ˀah} \textsc{int}-aunt/mother-{\diminutive}.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{etino̱há:ˀah} \trs{our aunt (two of us)} Different-generation kinship term, with reversed roles (meaning that the stem is inflected just like a same-generation kinship term)

\thematicheadword{aunt (to have a maternal aunt)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-no:ha:-ˀah} \textsc{p}-aunt/mother-{\diminutive}. Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gono:há:ˀah} \trs{her aunt}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hono:há:ˀah} \trs{his aunt} (on the maternal side)
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{sano:há:ˀah} \trs{your aunt}


\thematicheadword{aunt (maternal aunt), also a term of address} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{k-no:ha:-ˀah} \textsc{1s.a}-mother/aunt-{\diminutive}. This word only takes \stem{k-} \exsc{1s.a}, which means \trs{my maternal relative}.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kno:há:ˀah} \trs{my aunt} (maternal), \trs{Auntie}

\thematicheadword{aunt (to have an aunt)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-haˀk} \textsc{p}-aunt. Archaic word, possibly related to verb ga̱haˀk \trs{it did sit on something}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{agé:haˀk} \trs{my aunt}


\thematicheadword{boyfriend -- see \trs{friend}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgyá:tsih} \trs{my friend, my boyfriend, my girl friend}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{goksaˀgowáhsraˀ} \trs{her boyfriend}

\thematicheadword{brother -- see sibling}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{deyagyadęhnǫ́:de:ˀ} \trs{my brother, my sister}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hehjíˀah} \trs{my older brother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{heˀgę́:ˀęh} \trs{my younger brother}

\thematicheadword{brother-in-law -- see \trs{in-law}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgyá:gyoh} \trs{my brother-in-law, sister-in-law}


\thematicheadword{child} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-hawahk-(shǫˀ)} \textsc{int}-child-({\pluralizer}). Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hehá:wahk} \trs{my son}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kehá:wahk} \trs{my daughter}

\thematicheadword{cousin (term of address)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-aˀse:ˀ} \textsc{int}-doubled.{\stative}. Same-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gya̱ˀse:ˀ} \trs{cousin!} (when directly addressing a cousin)
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gwa̱ˀse:ˀ} \trs{cousins} (when directly addressing one’s cousins)

\thematicheadword{cousin} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-aˀse:ˀ} \textsc{p}-doubled.{\stative}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{honáˀse:ˀ} \trs{his cousin}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgyáˀse:ˀ} \trs{my cousin}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgwaˀsé:ˀsǫˀ} \trs{our cousins}

\thematicheadword{cousin (to have a cousin)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-aˀse:ˀ-shęˀ} \textsc{p}-doubled.{\stative}-\textsc{declare}.-\textsc{kinship}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgyaˀsé:ˀshęˀ} \trs{our cousins}


\thematicheadword{daughter -- see ‘child'}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kehá:wahk} \trs{my daughter}

\thematicheadword{daughter-in-law} -- see \trs{in-law}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kehsé:yǫh} \trs{daughter-in-law}

\thematicheadword{family} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-hwajiy-aˀ} \textsc{p}-family-{\nsf}. Noun functioning as a kinship term.}\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{akwa:jí:yaˀ} \trs{my family}

\thematicheadword{father} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-ˀnih} \textsc{int}-father. Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hǫwáˀnih} \trs{his/her father}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hya̱ˀnih} \trs{your father}

\thematicheadword{father (have a father)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-ˀnih-shęˀ} \textsc{p}-father-{\declarekinship}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hoˀníshęˀ} \trs{he has a father}

\thematicheadword{father (also a term of address)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{ha -ˀnih} \textsc{3s.m.a}-father. This word only takes \stem{ha-} \exsc{3s.m.a}, which means \trs{my paternal relative}.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{haˀnih} \trs{my father}, \trs{Dad}

\columnbreak
\thematicheadword{father-in-law -- see \trs{in-law}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hakné:nhǫ:s} \trs{my father-in-law}

\thematicheadword{friend (term of address)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-atsih} \textsc{int}-paired.{\stative}. Same-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gyá:tsih} \trs{Friend} (term of address)

\thematicheadword{friend (ceremonial friend)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-ad-aoˀ-(shǫˀ)} \textsc{p}-{\semireflexive}-run.{\stative}-({\pluralizer}). Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{honadáoˀsǫˀ} \trs{they are friends}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hona:dáoˀ} \trs{his ceremonial friend}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgwadáoˀsǫˀ} \trs{we all are buddies, friends}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgwá:daoˀ} \trs{we all are friends}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgyá:daoˀ} \trs{my ceremonial friend}, \trs{Friend} (term of address)


\thematicheadword{friend (have, become a ceremonial friend)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-ad-aoˀ-tsę:ˀ} \textsc{p}-{\semireflexive}-run.{\stative}-{\declarekinship}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{agaǫdáoˀtsę:ˀ} \trs{they (fe/males) became ceremonial friends}


\thematicheadword{friend} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-atsih} \textsc{p}-paired.{\stative}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{goná:tsih} \trs{they (animals) are friends}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{honá:tsih} \trs{his friend}, \trs{they (males) are friends}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgyá:tsih} \trs{my friend}, \trs{my boyfriend}, \trs{my girl friend}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgwátsi̱hsǫˀ} \trs{all of our friends}


\thematicheadword{friend, boyfriend, girlfriend} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-ksaˀgowahsr-aˀ} \textsc{int}-beautiful.one-{\nsf}. Same-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{heksaˀgowáhsraˀ} \trs{my boyfriend}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{keksaˀgowáhsraˀ} \trs{my girlfriend}


\thematicheadword{friend, boyfriend, girlfriend} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-ksaˀgowahsr-aˀ} \textsc{p}-beautiful.one-{\nsf}. Noun functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hoksaˀgohwáhsraˀ} \trs{his girlfriend}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ageksaˀgówa̱hsraˀ} \trs{my boyfriend}, \trs{my girlfriend}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{goksaˀgowáhsraˀ} \trs{her boyfriend}

\thematicheadword{girlfriend -- see \trs{friend}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgyá:tsih} \trs{my friend}, \trs{my boyfriend}, \trs{my girlfriend}


\thematicheadword{godfather (also a term of address). Also see \trs{stepfather}} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{ha-ˀni:hah} \textsc{3s.m.a}-step-father. This word only takes \stem{ha-} \exsc{3s.m.a}, which means \trs{my paternal relative}.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{haˀní:hah} \trs{my godfather}

\thematicheadword{grandchild} \stem{\textsc{int}-adreˀ-(shǫˀ)} 
\CayugaRemark{\textsc{int}-grandchild-({\pluralizer}). Different-gener\-ation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gakéyadreˀ} \trs{my grandchildren (said when reckoning a bloodline)}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{heyá:dreˀ} \trs{my grandson}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hagá:dreˀ} \trs{his grandson, me}, \trs{I am his grandson}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{keyá:dreˀ} \trs{my granddaughter}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hǫwá:dreˀ} \trs{his/her grandson}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{sheyá:dreˀ} \trs{your granddaughter}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫdádadreˀ} \trs{her granddaughter}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{shagó:dreˀ} \trs{his granddaughter}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gaǫdadreˀsǫ́:ˀah} \trs{her grandchildren}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{shagódre̱ˀsǫˀ} \trs{his grandchildren}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gakeyadréˀsǫˀ} \trs{my grandchildren}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gasheyadréˀsǫˀ} \trs{your grandchildren}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hehsá:dreˀ} \trs{your grandson}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gwadre:ˀ} \trs{you are my grandchild} (term of address)


\thematicheadword{grandmother -- see \trs{grandparent}}\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgéhso:t} \trs{my grandmother}

\thematicheadword{grandmother (to have a grandmother)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-hsot} \textsc{p}-grandparent. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hohso:t} \trs{his Grandma}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{sa̱hso:t} \trs{your Grandmother}

\thematicheadword{grandmother (also a term of address; also a term of respect for an old woman)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{k-hsot} \textsc{1s.a}-grand.parent. This word only takes \linebreak \stem{k-} \exsc{1s.a}, which means \trs{my maternal relative}.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kso:t} \trs{my grandmother}, \trs{Grandma}


\thematicheadword{grandparent} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-hsot} \textsc{int}-grandparent. Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hehso:t} \trs{my grandfather}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgéhso:t} \trs{my grandmother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{etíhso:t} \trs{our grandmother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hagéhso:t, hakso:t} \trs{my grandfather}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hǫwáhso:t} \trs{his/her grandfather}

\thematicheadword{grandparent (to have a grandparent)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-hsot-shęˀ} \textsc{p}-grandparent-{\declarekinship}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hohsó:tsęˀ} \trs{he has a grandparent}


\thematicheadword{grandson -- see \trs{grandchild}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{heyá:dreˀ} \trs{my grandson}

\thematicheadword{husband -- see \trs{spouse}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hegę́hjih} \trs{my husband}

\thematicheadword{in-law, sibling-in-law} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-agyoh} \textsc{p}-sibling.in.law. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{goná:gyoh} \trs{their (fe/males) or her in-laws}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{honá:gyoh} \trs{their (males) in-laws}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgyá:gyoh} \trs{my brother-in-law}, \trs{my sister-in-law}


\thematicheadword{in-law (a female’s mother/daughter-in-law)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-hseyǫh} \textsc{int}-female.sex-in.law. Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgehsé:yǫh} \trs{my mother-in-law} (a woman speaking)
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kehsé:yǫh} \trs{my daughter-in-law} (a woman speaking)

\thematicheadword{in-law (a female’s mother/daughter-in-law)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-hseyǫh} \textsc{int}-female.sex-in.law. Same-generation kinship term.}\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫdade̱hsé:yǫh} \trs{her mother-in-law}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{shehsé:yǫh} \trs{your mother-in-law, your daughter-in-law} (when speaking to a woman)


\thematicheadword{in-law (someone’s father/son-in-law, a male’s mother-in-law)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-nenhǫs} \textsc{int}-in.law. Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hené:nhǫ:s} \trs{my son-in-law}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hakné:nhǫ:s} \trs{my father-in-law}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hyané:nhǫ:s} \trs{your father-in-law}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫkné:nhǫ:s} \trs{my mother-in-law} (a man speaking)

\thematicheadword{in-law (someone’s father/son-in-law, a male’s mother-in-law)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-nenhǫs} \textsc{int}-different.sex.in.law. Same-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hǫwánenhǫ:s} \trs{his/her father-in-law, son-in-law}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{shagónenhǫ:s} \trs{his mother-in-law}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{shené:nhǫ:s} \trs{your mother-in-law} (when speaking to a man)


\thematicheadword{kin -- see \trs{relatives}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫdę́nǫ̱hksǫˀ} \trs{relatives, kin,} etc.

\thematicheadword{mother} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-no:haˀ} \textsc{int}-mother. Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{etíno̱haˀ} \trs{our mother}, \trs{women}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫdátno̱haˀ} \trs{her mother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫkíno̱haˀ} \trs{our mother}


\thematicheadword{mother (to have a mother)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-no:haˀ} \textsc{p}-mother. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{honó:haˀ} \trs{his mother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{sanó:haˀ} \trs{your mother}

\thematicheadword{mother (to have a mother)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-no:haˀ-tsęˀ} \textsc{p}-mother-{\declarekinship}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{honó:haˀtsęˀ} \trs{he has a mother}

\thematicheadword{mother (also a term of address)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{k-nó:haˀ} \textsc{1s.a}-mother. This word only takes \stem{k-} \exsc{1s.a}, which means \trs{my maternal relative}.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{knó:haˀ} \trs{my mother}, \trs{Mom}

\thematicheadword{mother-in-law -- see in-law}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫkné:nhǫ:s} \trs{my (a male’s) mother-in-law}


\thematicheadword{neighbour} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{de-\textsc{a}-nǫhsa-kahǫh} {\dualic}-\textsc{a}-house-adjoin.{\stative}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{deswęnǫ̱hsákahǫˀ} \trs{your neighbour}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{swęnǫhsakáhǫˀ} \trs{your neighbours}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{deyagwanǫhsakáhǫh} \trs{my neighbours}

\thematicheadword{neighbour} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{de-\textsc{a}-nǫhsa-ne:gę:} {\dualic}-\textsc{a}-house-side.by.side.{\stative}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{deyagyanǫhsané:gę:} \trs{my neighbour}


\thematicheadword{nephew, niece} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-ǫhwadęˀ-(shǫˀ)} \textsc{int}-nephew/niece-({\pluralizer}). Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{heyǫ́hwa:dęˀ} \trs{my nephew}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gakeyǫ̱hwá:dęˀ} \trs{my brother’s children}, \trs{my nieces and nephews}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{keyǫhwá:dęˀ} \trs{my niece}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gasheyǫ̱hwádę̱ˀsǫˀ} \trs{your nieces and nephews}

\thematicheadword{nephew, niece} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-yaˀdawęh} \textsc{int}-support.someone.{\stative}. Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gakeya̱ˀdá:węh} \trs{my brother’s \newline children}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{heyáˀdawęh} \trs{my nephew}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{keyáˀdawęh} \trs{my brother’s kids}

\thematicheadword{niece -- see \trs{nephew, niece}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{keyǫhwá:dęˀ} \trs{my niece}


\thematicheadword{niece (maternal)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-hawa:k-ˀah} \textsc{int}-child-{\diminutive}. The child of one’s mother’s brother or mother’s sister. Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫdatawá:kˀah} \trs{her maternal niece}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kehawá:kˀah} \trs{my maternal niece}

\thematicheadword{relative (to be mutually related)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{a}-adę-nǫhk-(shǫˀ)} \textsc{a}-{\semireflexive}-mutually.related-({\pluralizer}). Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{agwadęnǫ́hksǫˀ} \trs{we are relatives}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{agya:dę́:nǫhk} \trs{my relative}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫdę́nǫ̱hksǫˀ} \trs{relatives, kin, etc}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgwadęnǫ́hksǫˀ} \trs{our relatives}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{sadę́:nohk} \trs{any relative (of yours)}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ja:dę́:nǫhk} \trs{your relative}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gęna:dę́:nǫhk} \trs{they (animals) are related}


\thematicheadword{relative (to have relatives on one’s father’s side)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-adę-ni-ho:nǫˀ} \textsc{p}-{\semireflexive}-father-{\populative}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{sadęni̱hó:nǫˀ} \trs{your relatives on your (sg) father’s side}

\thematicheadword{relative (close relative)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-nǫhk-shǫˀ} \textsc{int}-love-{\pluralizer}. Same-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kenǫ́hksǫˀ} \trs{my relatives}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{shenǫ́hksǫˀ} \trs{your relatives} (high language, describing our relationship to all people)

\thematicheadword{relative}
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-ǫgweˀd-aˀ} \textsc{p}-people-{\nounstemformer}. Noun functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{agǫ́gwe̱ˀdaˀ} \trs{my relatives}


\thematicheadword{sibling (near in age)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{de-\textsc{a}-adę-hnǫde:ˀ} {\dualic}-\textsc{a}-{\semireflexive}-follow.{\stative}, \stem{de-\textsc{a}-adę-hnǫd-rǫˀ} {\dualic}-\textsc{a}-\exsc{\semireflexive}-follow-{\distributive.\stative}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{degadę̱hnǫ́:de:ˀ} \trs{my sibling}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{degaǫdęhnǫ́:de:ˀ} \trs{his/her sister}, \trs{his/her brother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{de̱hęnadęhnǫ́:de:ˀ} \trs{his brother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{de̱hęnadęhnǫ́:drǫˀ} \trs{his brothers}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{deyagyadęhnǫ́:de:ˀ} \trs{my brother, my sister} (my nearest sibling)

\thematicheadword{sibling (younger)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-ˀgę:ˀęh} \textsc{int}-younger.sibling. Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{heˀgę́:ˀęh} \trs{my younger brother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hesheˀgę́:ˀęh} \trs{your younger brother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kheˀgę́:ˀęh} \trs{my younger sister}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫdade̱ˀgę́:ˀęh} \trs{her younger sister}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{shagoˀgę́:ˀęh} \trs{his younger sister}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hǫwaˀgę́:ˀęh} \trs{his / her younger brother}\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{sheˀgę́:ˀęh} \trs{your younger sister}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{etiˀgę́:ˀah} \trs{our younger sister}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{shǫgwaˀgę́:ˀah} \trs{our younger brother}

\thematicheadword{sibling (older)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{a}-ad-riyah-shǫˀ} \textsc{a}-{\semireflexive}-older.sibling-{\pluralizer}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gaǫdriyáhsǫˀ} \trs{my older siblings}

\thematicheadword{sibling (older)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{t-\textsc{a}-gowanę-ˀs} {\cislocative}-\textsc{a}-big.{\stative}-{\plural}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{tgaegówanęˀs} \trs{my older siblings}


\thematicheadword{sibling (older)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-hji-ˀah} \textsc{int}-older.sibling-{\diminutive}. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hehshéhjiˀah} \trs{your older brother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kehjíˀah} \trs{my older sister}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hehjíˀah} \trs{my older brother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hǫwáhji̱ˀah} \trs{his older brother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gakehjiˀáhsǫˀ} \trs{my older brothers, sisters}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{shedwáhjiˀah} \trs{our older brother}

\thematicheadword{sibling (to have an older sibling)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-hjiˀah} \textsc{p}-older.sibling. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hohjíˀah} \trs{his older sister}. 

\thematicheadword{sibling-in-law -- see \trs{in-law}}
\pseudoitem{} ǫgyá:gyoh ‘my brother-in-law, sister-in-law

\thematicheadword{sister -- see \trs{sibling}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kehjíˀah} \trs{my older sister}


\thematicheadword{sister (also a term of address)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{k-hehjiˀ} \textsc{1s.a}-older.sibling}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kehjiˀ} \trs{my older sister} (term of address for one’s relative)

\thematicheadword{sister-in-law -- see \trs{in-law}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫgyá:gyoh} \trs{my brother-in-law}, \trs{my sister-in-law}

\thematicheadword{son -- see \trs{child}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hehá:wahk} \trs{my son}


\thematicheadword{son-in-law -- see \trs{in-law}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hené:nhǫ:s} \trs{my son-in-law}

\thematicheadword{spouse, husband, wife (informal)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-gęhjih} \textsc{int}-old.person.{\stative}. Same-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hegę́hjih} \trs{my husband}, \trs{my old man}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kegę́hjih} \trs{my wife}, \trs{my old lady}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hehségę̱hjih} \trs{your husband}, \trs{your old man}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{shegę́hjih} \trs{your wife}, \trs{your old lady}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hǫwágę̱hjih} \trs{her husband}, \trs{her old man}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{shagógę̱hjih} \trs{his wife}, \trs{his old lady}


\thematicheadword{spouse, husband, wife} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{de-\textsc{a}-(r)ihwawaˀkǫˀ} {\dualic}-\textsc{a}-support.{\stative} \newline Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{degaeihwawáˀkǫˀ} \trs{her husband}, \trs{his wife}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{desni:hwáwaˀkǫˀ} \trs{your wife}, \trs{your husband}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{deyakni:hwawáˀkǫ:ˀ} \trs{my wife}, \trs{my husband}

\thematicheadword{spouse, husband, wife} Atypical word.
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ho:ˀ} \trs{his or her spouse}


\thematicheadword{step-daughter -- see \trs{step-relative}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫdá:tno:ˀ} \trs{her step-daughter}

\thematicheadword{step-father -- see step-relative}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hakno:ˀ} \trs{my step-father}

\thematicheadword{step-father, god-father} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-ˀni:-hah} \textsc{int}-father-{\diminutive}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hǫwaˀní:hah} \trs{her godfather}, \trs{her step-father}


\thematicheadword{step-father, god-father (also a term of address)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{ha-ˀni:-hah} \textsc{3s.m.a}-father-{\diminutive}. This word only takes \stem{ha-} \exsc{3s.m.a}, which means \trs{my paternal relative}.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{haˀní:hah} \trs{my godfather}

\thematicheadword{step-mother} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-naˀehs} \textsc{int}-step.mother. Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫknáˀehs} \trs{my step-mother}

\thematicheadword{step-mother (to have a step-mother)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-naˀehs} \textsc{p}-step.mother. \newline  Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{aknáˀehs} \trs{I have a step-mother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{sanáˀehs} \trs{your step-mother}

\thematicheadword{step-mother (to have a step-mother)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{p}-no:ˀ} \textsc{p}-step.parent/child. Verb functioning as a kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{sano:ˀ} \trs{your step-mother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hono:ˀ} \trs{his step-mother}


\thematicheadword{step-relative} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-no:ˀ} \textsc{int}-step.parent/child. Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫkno:ˀ} \trs{my step-mother}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hǫwá:no:ˀ} \trs{her step-son}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{ǫdá:tno:ˀ} \trs{her step-daughter}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hakno:ˀ} \trs{my step-father}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hehsno:ˀ} \trs{your step-son}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{shé:no:ˀ} \trs{your step-daughter}

\thematicheadword{step-son -- see \trs{step-relative}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hǫwá:no:ˀ} \trs{her step-son}

\thematicheadword{uncle (maternal uncle)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-noˀsęh} \textsc{int}-maternal.uncle. This word used to refer only to one’s mother’s brother. Now, it can also refer to one’s father’s brother. Different-generation kinship term.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gakéno̱ˀsęh} \trs{my uncles}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{gakeno̱ˀsęhsǫ́:ˀǫh} \trs{my uncles}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{haknóˀsęh} \trs{he is my uncle}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hǫwáno̱ˀsęh} \trs{his/her uncle}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hyanóˀsęh} \trs{your uncle}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{shǫkníno̱ˀsęh} \trs{our uncle}

\thematicheadword{uncle (maternal uncle)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{\textsc{int}-noˀsęh} \textsc{int}-maternal.uncle. Different-generation kinship term, with reversed roles (meaning that the stem is inflected just like a same-generation kinship term).}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{Etino̱ˀsę́h Ęhęnatnoˀáowanaht} \trs{Our Uncles ceremony} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{eti-no̱ˀsę́h} \textsc{1ns.in>3s.fi/3ns}-uncle \cayuga{Ęhęnatnoˀáowanaht} \trs{they become Bigheads}}

\thematicheadword{uncle (also a term of address)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{k-noˀsęh} \textsc{1s.a}-maternal.uncle. This word only takes \stem{k-} 1s.a, which means \trs{my maternal relative}.}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{knóˀsęh} \trs{my uncle}

\thematicheadword{wife -- see \trs{spouse}}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{kegę́hjih} \trs{my wife}


\end{multicols}


\section{Terms of address} \label{terms of address (list)}
\begin{multicols}{2}
%\pseudoitem{}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Doda} \trs{Grandparent!}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Doda Grace} \trs{Great Aunt Grace!} (term of address for maternal great aunt)
\lexicalunitnobracket{Dodama:} \trs{Grandma!} (term of address for grandmother on one’s mother’s side)
\lexicalunitnobracket{Gwadre:ˀ} \trs{Grandchild!} (term of address said by any old person to a young child)
\lexicalunitnobracket{Gwaˀse:} \trs{Cousins!} (term of address used when directly speaking to one’s cousins)
\lexicalunitnobracket{Gyá:tsi:} \trs{Friend!}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Gyaˀse:} \trs{Cousin!} (term of address used when directly speaking to a cousin)
\lexicalunitnobracket{Hehjiˀ} \trs{Older brother!}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Hehso:t} \trs{Grandpa!}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Kehjiˀ} \trs{Older sister!}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Kso:t} \trs{Grandmother!} (term of address; also a term of respect for an old person)
\lexicalunitnobracket{Kno:há:ˀah} \trs{maternal aunt}, \trs{Auntie!}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Knó:haˀ} \trs{Mom!}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Knoˀsęh} \trs{Uncle!}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Kso:t} \trs{Grandma!}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Sgę:nǫ́: Degyadęhnǫ́:de:ˀ} \trs{Hello brother / sister!} (Said by a brother, sister, if they haven’t seen each other in a while.)
\lexicalunitnobracket{Yahso:t} \trs{Grandparent!}, \trs{Grandchild!} (term of address; also a term of \linebreak  endearment).
\end{multicols}

\section{Clans} \label{clans}
\thematicheadword{Introductions}
\largerpage
\ea
\gll Dę́ˀ ni:s hoˀdę́ˀ nisaˀsyáoˀdę:?\\
what you kind your.kind.of.clan\\
\glt \trs{What is your clan?}
\cfex{ni-sa-ˀsya-oˀdę: {\partitive}-\textsc{2s.p}-clan-type.of.{\stative}}
\z

\ea 
\gll Dę́ˀ ni:s hoˀdę́ˀ niswaˀsyáoˀdę:?\\
what you kind your.kind.of.clan\\
\glt \trs{What is your clan?} (The speaker knows you are all of the same family.)
\cfex{ni-swa-ˀsya-oˀdę: {\partitive}-\textsc{2p.p}-clan-type.of.{\stative}}
\z 

\ea 
\gll Dę́ˀ ni:s hoˀdę́ˀ niswaˀsyáoˀdę:ˀs?\\
what you kind your.kinds.of.clan\\
\glt \trs{What is your clan?} (The speaker knows that you are from different families.)
\cfex{ni-swa-ˀsya-oˀdę:-ˀs {\partitive}-\textsc{2p.p}-clan-type.of.{\stative}-{\plural}}
\z 

\ea 
\gll …  niwage̱ˀsyáoˀdę:, nǫgeˀsyáoˀdę:.\\
{} my.kind.of.clan {}\\
\glt \trs{I am … clan.}
\cfex{ni-wag-e-ˀsyá-oˀdę: {\partitive}-\exsc{1s.p-{\joinerE}}-clan-type.of.{\stative}}
\cfex{nǫg-e-ˀsyá-oˀdę: \exsc{\partitive.1s.p-{\joinerE}}-clan-type.of.{\stative}}
\z


\begin{multicols}{2}

\pseudoitem{} hadihnyagwái ga̱ˀsyá:deˀ \trs{they (males) are Bear Clan}

\section{Nations} \label{nations}

\thematicheadword{Introductions}

\z

I.am.a.real.person I\\

\z

a.real.person I\\

\gll Gayogo̱hó:nǫˀ ni:ˀ.\\
Cayuga I\\

\cfex{ni-wag-o̱hęts-oˀdę: {\partitive}-\textsc{1s.p}-nation-kind.of.{\stative}}
\z

\begin{multicols}{2}

\thematicheadword{Aboriginal}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Ǫgwehǫ́:weh} \trs{First Nations}, \trs{Indian people}, \trs{Aboriginal}, \trs{Native}

\subsection{Miscellaneous}
Thematic headwords (in small caps) either provide the English name for a hereditary chief position or constitute a thematic category for grouping related words.

\section{Weekdays} \label{weekdays}
The following terms are arranged approximately in chronological order.
\begin{multicols}{2}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Awędędáˀǫh} \trs{Monday} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{aw-ęd-ędáˀ-ǫh} \textsc{3s.p}-day-end-{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Ęwę:dę́:daˀ} \trs{next Monday}
\CayugaRemark{\stem{ę-w-ę:d-ę́:da-ˀ} {\future}-\textsc{3s.a}-day-end-{\punctual}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Tsaˀwę:dę́:daˀ} \trs{last Monday} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{tsaˀ-w-ę:d-ę́:da-ˀ} \exsc{\coincident.\factual-3s.a}-day-end-{\punctual}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Dekní: hadǫˀt} \trs{Tuesday} \cayuga{dekní:} \trs{two} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{hadǫˀt} day.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Dekní: hadǫ́ˀtgę̱hę:ˀ} \trs{last Tuesday} \cayuga{dekní:} \trs{two} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{hadǫˀt-gę̱hę:ˀ} day.{\stative}-{\former}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Ahsę́h Hadǫˀt} \trs{Wednesday} \cayuga{ahsę́h} \trs{three}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Ahsę́h Hadǫ́ˀtgę̱hę:ˀ} \trs{last Wednesday}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Geí: Hadǫˀt} \trs{Thursday} \cayuga{geí} \trs{four}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Geí: Hadǫ́ˀtgę̱hę:ˀ} \trs{last Thursday} 
\lexicalunitnobracket{Hwíhs Hadǫˀt} \trs{Friday} \cayuga{hwíhs} \trs{five}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Hwíhs Hadǫ́ˀtgę̱hę:ˀ} \trs{last Friday}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Nakdóhaehs} \trs{Saturday} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{nakd-ohae-hs} bed-wash-{\habitual}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Hejonakdóhaehs} \trs{next Saturday} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{hej-o-nakd-óhae-hs} {\translocative}.{\repetitive}-\textsc{3s.p}-bed-wash-{\habitual}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Tsiyonakdóhaehs} \trs{last Saturday} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{tsi-yo-nakd-óhae-hs} {\coincident}-\textsc{3s.p}-bed-wash-{\habitual}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Awędadógę̱hdǫh} \trs{Sunday} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{aw-ęda-dógę̱hd-ǫh} \textsc{3s.p}-day-make.true-\stative}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Ęyaǫdadogę́hteˀ} \trs{next Sunday}  
\CayugaRemark{\stem{ę-ya-ǫda-dogę́hd-h-e-ˀ} {\future}-\textsc{3s.p}-day-make.true-{\dislocative}-go-{\punctual}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Tsiyaǫdadogę́hdǫh} \trs{last Sunday} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{tsi-ya-ǫda-dogę́hd-ǫh} {\coincident}-\textsc{3s.p}-day-make.true-{\stative}}

\CayugaRemark{\stem{o-yá-ˀ} \textsc{3s.p}-other-{\nsf} \stem{deg-y-ad-ǫdá:-dr-e-hk} {\dualic}.{\cislocative}-\exsc{3s.p}-{\semireflexive}-day-{\dislocative}-go.{\stative}-{\former}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{shęh naˀdegyadǫ́dai} \trs{during the week} 
\CayugaRemark{\cayuga{shęh} \trs{that} \stem{naˀdeg-y-ad-ǫ́da-i} {\partitive}-{\dualic}-{\cislocative}-\textsc{3s.a}-{\semireflexive}-day-stuck.on.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Tsaˀdegyadǫdaí, tsaˀdegyadǫ́dadeˀ} \trs{during the week} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{tsaˀdeg-y-ad-ǫda-í} \exsc{\coincident.\dualic.\cislocative-3s.p}-{\semireflexive}-day-stuck.\-on.{\stative} \stem{tsaˀdeg-y-ad-ǫ́d-adeˀ} \exsc{\coincident.\dualic.\cislocative-3s.p}-{\semireflexive}-day-exist.{\stative}}

\CayugaRemark{\cayuga{sgá:t} \trs{one} \stem{ę-ya-ǫda-dogéhd-h-e-ˀ} {\future}-\textsc{3s.p}-day-make.true-{\dislocative}-go-{\punctual}}

\CayugaRemark{\stem{tsaˀdeg-y-ad-ǫ́da-dr-e-hk} \exsc{\coincident.\dualic.\cislocative-3s.p}-day-{\dislocative}-go-{\former}}

\section{Clock time} \label{clock time}
The following information is largely from chapter 9 of \citet[99--120]{mithun_watewayestanih_1984}. The following terms are arranged approximately in chronological order.
\begin{multicols}{2}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Do: niyowi̱hsdáˀe:?} \trs{What time is it?} 
\CayugaRemark{\cayuga{do:} \trs{how} \stem{ni-yo-wi̱hsd-á-ˀe:} {\partitive}-\textsc{3s.p}-metal-{\joinerA}-strike.{\stative}}
\subsection{On the hour}
\lexicalunitnobracket{sgá:t jo̱hwíhsda̱ˀe:, sgá:t ohwíhsda̱ˀe:} \trs{one o’clock} 
\CayugaRemark{\cayuga{sga:t} \trs{one} \stem{(j)-o̱-hwíhsd-a̱-ˀe:} \exsc{(\repetitive)-3s.p}-metal-{\joinerA}-strike.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{dekní: dejohwi̱hsdáˀe:, dekní: deyohwi̱hsdáˀe:} \trs{two o’clock} 
\CayugaRemark{\cayuga{dekni:} \trs{two} \stem{de(j)-o-hwi̱hsd-á-ˀe:} {\dualic}.\exsc{\repetitive)-3s.p}-metal-{\joinerA}-strike.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{ahsę́ niyohwi̱hsdáˀe:} \trs{three o’clock} 
\CayugaRemark{\cayuga{ahsę́} \trs{three} \stem{ni-yo-hwi̱hsd-á-ˀe:} {\partitive}-\textsc{3s.p}-metal-{\joinerA}-strike.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{géi niyohswi̱hsdáˀe:} \trs{four o’clock} \cayuga{géi} \trs{four}
\lexicalunitnobracket{ja:dáhk niyohwi̱hsdáˀe:} \trs{seven o’clock} \cayuga{ja:dáhk} \trs{seven}
\lexicalunitnobracket{gyo̱hdǫ́: niyohwi̱hsdáˀe:} \trs{nine o’clock} \cayuga{gyo̱hdǫ́:} \trs{nine}
\lexicalunitnobracket{sgá:t sga̱héˀ niyohwi̱hsdáˀe:} \trs{eleven o’clock} \cayuga{sga:t sga̱héˀ} \trs{eleven}
\lexicalunitnobracket{dekní: sga̱héˀ niyohwi̱hsdáˀe:} \trs{twelve o’clock} \cayuga{dekní: sga̱héˀ} \trs{twelve}
\lexicalunitnobracket{jo̱hwihsda̱ˀehne:ˀ} \trs{it was one o’clock} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{j-o̱-hwihsd-a̱-ˀe-hne:ˀ} {\repetitive}-\textsc{3s.p}-metal-{\joinerA}-strike.{\stative}-{\remote}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{dekni: deyohwi̱hsdaˀehne:ˀ} \trs{it was two o’clock} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{de-yo-hwi̱hsd-a-ˀe-hne:ˀ} {\dualic}-\textsc{3s.p}-metal-{\joinerA}-strike.{\stative}-{\remote}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{ahsęh niyohwi̱hsdaˀehne:ˀ} \trs{it was three o’clock} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{ni-yo-hwi̱hsd-a-ˀe-hne:ˀ} {\partitive}-\textsc{3s.p}-metal-{\joinerA}-strike.{\stative}-{\remote}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{tsaˀdegáǫhya̱hęh / gáǫhya̱hęh} \trs{noon} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{tsaˀde-gá-ǫhy-a̱-hęh} \exsc{(\coincident.\dualic)-3s.a}-sky-{\joinerA}-mid.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{tsaˀdewa̱hsǫ́:tęh / wa̱hsǫ́:tęh} \trs{midnight} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{tsaˀde-w-a̱hsǫ́:d-hęh} {\coincident}.{\dualic}-\textsc{3s.a}-night-mid.{\stative}}
\subsection{Past the hour}
\lexicalunitnobracket{tsaˀdeyo̱hsę:nǫ́ˀ niyodǫgóhdǫh} \trs{it is half-past the hour} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{tsaˀde-yo̱-\newline hs-ę:nǫ́-ˀ} {\coincident}.{\dualic}-\textsc{3s.p}-noun-originate.from-{\stative} \stem{ni-yo-d-ǫgóhd-ǫh} {\partitive}-\exsc{3s.p}-{\semireflexive}-surpass-{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{odǫgo̱hdǫ́} \trs{it has gone beyond, surpassed} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{o-d-ǫgo̱hd-ǫ́h} \exsc{3s.p}-{\semireflexive}-surpass-{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{tsaˀdewa̱hsę:nǫ́ odǫgo̱hdǫ́ sgá:t} \trs{half past one} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{tsaˀde-wa̱-hs-ę:nǫ́-h} {\coincident}.{\dualic}-\textsc{3s.a}-noun-originate.from-{\habitual}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{tsáˀdewa̱hsę:nǫ́ odǫgo̱hdǫ́ dekni:} \trs{half past two}
\lexicalunitnobracket{tsáˀdewa̱hsę:nǫ́ odǫgo̱hdǫ́ ahsęh} \trs{half past three}
\lexicalunitnobracket{tsáˀdewa̱hsę:nǫ́ odǫgo̱hdǫ́ degrǫˀ} \trs{half past eight}
\lexicalunitnobracket{tsáˀdewa̱hsę:nǫ́ odǫgo̱hdǫ́ sgá:t sga̱heˀ} \trs{half past eleven}
\lexicalunitnobracket{hwíhs sga̱heˀ odǫgo̱hdǫ́ sgá:t} \trs{fifteen past one}, \trs{1:15} \cayuga{hwíhs sga̱heˀ} \trs{fifteen}
\lexicalunitnobracket{hwíhs odǫgo̱hdǫ́ ahsęh} \trs{five past three}, \trs{3:05}
\lexicalunitnobracket{dewahshę́: hwíhs odǫgo̱hdǫ́ géi} \trs{twenty-five past four}, \trs{4:25} 
\CayugaRemark{\cayuga{dewahshę́: hwíhs} \trs{twenty-five}}
\subsection{Before, to the hour}
\lexicalunitnobracket{gyonǫ́:haˀ} \trs{till}, \trs{to}, \trs{before}, \trs{of} (referring to time)
\CayugaRemark{\stem{g-yon-ǫ́:haˀ} {\cislocative}-\exsc{3p.p}-alone.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{wa̱hshę́: gyonǫ:háˀ sgá:t} \trs{12:50}, \trs{ten to one} 
\CayugaRemark{\cayuga{wa̱hshę́:} \trs{ten}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{dewahshę́: gyonǫ:háˀ hyéiˀ} \trs{5:40}, \trs{twenty to six} 
\CayugaRemark{\cayuga{dewahshę́:} \trs{twenty}, \cayuga{hyéiˀ} \trs{six}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{hwíhs sga̱héˀ gyonǫ:háˀ dekní: sga̱heˀ} \trs{11:45}, \trs{quarter to twelve} 
\CayugaRemark{\cayuga{hwíhs sga̱héˀ} \trs{fifteen}, \cayuga{dekní: sga̱heˀ} \trs{twelve}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{hwíhs gyonǫ:háˀ hyéiˀ} \trs{5:55}, \trs{five to six}
\lexicalunitnobracket{hwihshǫˀ, hwihshǫ:ˀǫh} \trs{five-ish}, \trs{around five o’clock)} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{hwihshǫˀ, hwihshǫ:ˀǫh}}

\end{multicols}

\CayugaRemark{\stem{o-nę́ˀd-agǫ:} \textsc{3s.p}-pine-\exsc{in}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Onǫ́dageh} \trs{Onondaga, ON} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{o-nǫ́d-aˀgeh} \textsc{3s.p}-hill-{\on}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Oswé:gęˀ} \trs{town of Ohsweken, Six Nations, ON} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{o-swé:gęˀ} \textsc{3s.p}-place.name}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Oˀnéhsagǫ:} \trs{In the Sand} (a place near Tonawanda, NY) 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{o-ˀnéhs-agǫ:} \textsc{3s.p}-sand-\exsc{in}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Sganędá:dih} \trs{Albany, NY} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{s-ga-nęˀd-á:dih} {\repetitive}-\textsc{3s.a}-pine-side.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Sganyada:digó:wah} \trs{Europe} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{s-ga-nyada:-adi-gó:wah} {\repetitive}-\textsc{3s.a}-lake-side.{\stative}-{\augmentative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Sganyádaes} \trs{Long Lake, NY}, \trs{Skaneateles, NY}  
\CayugaRemark{\stem{s-ga-nyáda-es} {\repetitive}-\textsc{3s.a}-lake-long.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Sgihę́:dih} \trs{Pinewoods, Cattaraugus, NY}  
\CayugaRemark{\stem{s-gihę́:-dih} {\repetitive}-\textsc{3s.a}.river-side{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Tahnawá:deˀ} \trs{Tonawanda, NY}  
\CayugaRemark{\stem{t-ha-hnaw-á:deˀ} {\cislocative}-\textsc{3s.m.a}-running.water-exist.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Tayędané:gęˀ} \trs{Deseronto, NY}  
\CayugaRemark{\stem{t-ha-yęd-a-né:gęˀ} {\cislocative}-\textsc{3s.m.a}-firewood-{\joinerA}-side.by.side.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Tgagwę́:troˀ} \trs{York, ON}, \trs{Jarvis, ON}  
\CayugaRemark{\stem{t-ga-gwę́:tr-oˀ} {\cislocative}-\textsc{3s.a}-silt-submerged.{\stative}}

\CayugaRemark{\stem{(t)-ga̱-hnáw-ęht-haˀ} \exsc{(\cislocative)-3s.a}-running.water-flow-{\habitual}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Tganádaę:ˀ} \trs{Caledonia, ON}  
\CayugaRemark{\stem{t-ga-náda-ęˀ} {\cislocative}-\textsc{3s.a}-town-lie.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Tganadaęˀgó:wah} \trs{Philadelphia, PA} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{t-ga-nada-ęˀ-gó:wah} {\cislocative}-\textsc{3s.a}-town-lie.{\stative}-{\augmentative}}

\section{Greetings, origins, and affiliations} \label{ch:greetings, origins, and affiliations}

\subsection{Greetings, social expressions, leave-taking}
The following expressions are in no particular order.

\begin{multicols}{2}
%\pseudoitem{} 
Sgę́:nǫ.ˀ \trs{Hello.}\\
Gwé:. \trs{Hello.}\\
Háiˀ / háeˀ. \trs{Hello.} (a word attributed to Oneida or Tutelo)\\
Agada̱ˀgáideˀ. \trs{I feel well.}\\
Aknǫhǫkdá:nih. \trs{I am sick.}\\
Agatowínyǫ̱ˀse:. \trs{I have a cold.}\\
Aknǫˀá:nǫ̱hwa:s. \trs{I have a headache.}\\
Oyányragyeˀ. \trs{It is going well.}\\
Haoˀ dę̱ˀ nyoh. \trs{O.k. then.}\\
O:nę́h gi̱ˀ hyá:ˀ. \trs{So long for now.}, \trs{Good-bye.}\\
Nyá:węh. \trs{Thank you.}\\
Nyóh. \trs{You’re welcome.}, \trs{Alright.}, \trs{O.k.}\\
Noya:! \trs{Happy New Year!} This expression or greeting is used at the start of the New Year. (Knocking on someone’s door at New Year’s and saying “Noya:!” will reward you with some home-made doughnuts.)

\end{multicols}
\largerpage
\begin{multicols}{2}

\ea
\gll Sgę:nǫ́ˀ sweˀgéh tgǫgę́hne:ˀ!\\
hello long.ago I.saw.you\\
\glt \mbox{\trs{I haven’t seen you for a while!}}
\z

\ea
\gll Sgę́:nǫˀ gę́h?\\
well Q\\
\glt \trs{Are you well?}
\z

\ea
\gll Sgęnǫ́jih gę́h?\\
really.well Q\\
\glt \trs{Are you really well?}
\z

\ea
\gll Ęhę́ˀ sgę́:nǫˀ.\\
yes well\\
\glt \trs{I am fine thanks.}
\z

\ea
\gll Tę́ˀ, tę́ˀ desgę́:nǫˀ\\
no no not.well\\
\glt \trs{No, I am not well.}
\z

\largerpage
\ea
\gll Sgę:nǫ́ˀ genǫ́hdǫnyǫh.\\
well I.feel\\
\glt \trs{I am fine or healthy.}
\z

\ea
\gll Nęˀ neˀ í:s?\\
that it.is you\\
\glt \trs{What about you?}
\z

\ea
\gll Neˀ diˀ í:s?\\
the so you\\
\glt \trs{What about you?}
\z
\end{multicols}

\ea
\gll Dęˀ hoˀdę́ˀ niyohdǫ̱hǫ:gyeˀ?\\
what kind it.is.going.along.happening\\
\glt \trs{What's happening?}, \trs{How's it going?}
\z 

\ea
\gll Dęˀ hoˀdę́ˀ niságye̱haˀ?\\
what kind you.are.doing\\
\glt \trs{What are you doing?}
\z 

\ea 
\gll Sgę:nǫ:ˀǫ́h niwágagyę:\\
slowly I.am.doing.it\\
\glt \trs{I'm taking it easy.}
\z 

\ea
\gll Gitsgodagyéˀs tsǫ:\\
I'm.loafing.around just\\
\glt \trs{I'm taking it easy.}
\z





\subsection{Asking about jobs}


\ea 
\gll Saíhoˀdeˀ?\\
you.are.working\\
\glt \trs{Are you working?}
\z

\ea 
\gll Gaę hwaˀ nhǫ́: hejisaihoˀdeˀ?\\
which emphasis place you.work.there\\
\glt \trs{Where do you work?}
\z 

\ea 
\gll Dę́ˀ hniˀ hoˀdę́ˀ hoihoˀdéˀsro̱ˀdę:?\\
what and kind his.kind.of.work\\
\glt \trs{And what does he do?}
\z 

\ea 
\gll Hoihoˀdeˀsri:yó: giˀ ne:ˀ.\\
he.has.a.good.job just it.is\\
\glt \trs{He has a good job.}
\z



\subsection{Asking someone’s age}


\ea 
\gll Dó: nisohsriyáˀgǫh?\\
how how.you(one).have.crossed.winters\\
\glt \trs{How old are you?}
\z 

\ea 
\gll Dó: ní:s nidihsé:nǫ:?\\
how you where.you(one).originate.from\\
\glt \trs{How old are you?}
\z 

\ea  
\gll Dó: ní:s nidihswé:nǫ:?\\
how you where.you(all).originate.from\\
\glt \trs{How old are you?}
\z 

\ea  
\gll Ahsę́ niwáhshę: niwago̱hsríya̱ˀgǫh\\
three tens how.I.have.crossed.rivers\\
\glt \trs{I am thirty years old.}
\z 




\ea How to communicate one's age (Use numbers in place of the ellipses.)\\
\ea …niwago̱hsríya̱ˀgǫh \trs{I am … years old}
\cfex{ni-wag-o̱hsr-íya̱ˀg-ǫh {\partitive}-\textsc{1s.p}-winter-cross-{\stative}}
\ex … nisohsriyáˀgǫh \trs{you (singular) are ... years old}
\ex … nisnohsriyáˀgǫh \trs{you two are ... years old}
\ex … nijohsriyáˀgǫh \trs{you all are ... years old}
\ex … nihao̱hsríya̱ˀgǫh \trs{he is ... years old}
\ex … niyagaohsriyáˀgǫh \trs{she is ... years old}
\ex … niyono̱hsríya̱ˀgǫh \trs{they (animals) are ... years old}
\ex … nihono̱hsríya̱ˀgǫh \trs{they (males) are ... years old}
\ex … niyagonohsriyáˀgǫh \trs{they (females or mixed) are ... years old}
\z
\z

\ea Another way to to communicate one's age (Use numbers in place of the ellipses.)\\
\ea … nidihsé:nǫ: \trs{you (singular) are … years old}
\cfex{ni-di-hs-é:n-ǫ: {\partitive-\cislocative}-\textsc{2s.p}-originate.from-{\stative}}
\ex … nidihswé:nǫ: \trs{you (plural) are … years old}
\ex … nitawé:nǫ: \trs{he is … years old}
\ex … nigya:wé:nǫ: \trs{it is … years old}
\ex … nigyagawé:nǫ: \trs{she is … years old}
\ex … nigyagoné:nǫ: \trs{they (females or mixed) are … years old}
\ex … nito:né:nǫˀs \trs{they (males) are … years old} 
\z
\z




\subsection{Asking someone’s name}


%\pseudoitem{}
\ea 
\gll Dę́ˀ ní:s hoˀdę́ˀ sya:sǫh?\\
what you kind you.are.called\\
\glt \trs{What is your name?}
\z

\ea 
\gll Dę́ˀ ní:s hoˀdę́ˀ swayasǫ̱hǫ:nyǫh?\\
what you kind you.all.are.individually.called\\
\glt \trs{What are your names?} (Each individual’s name is requested)
\z

\ea 
\gll Dę́ˀ ní:s hoˀdę́ˀ swaya:sǫh?\\
what you kind you.all.are.called\\
\glt \trs{What is your name?} (Said, for example, when asking people for their team’s name)
\z

\ea 
\gll Dę́ˀ hoˀdę́ˀ eya:sǫ́h neˀ sanó:haˀ?\\
what kind she.is.called the your.mother\\
\glt \trs{What is your mother’s (female relative’s) name?}
\z

\ea 
\gll Í:ˀ gęh sgí:dǫh?\\
I Q you.mean.me\\
\glt \trs{Do you mean me?}
\z

\ea 
\gll Nę: ne:ˀ í:s?\\
this it.is me\\
\glt \trs{What about you?}
\z

\ea 
\gll John ní:ˀ gyá:sǫh.\\
John I I.am.called\\
\glt \trs{My name is John.}
\z



\ea naming someone (use names in place of the ellipses)\\
\ea … gyá:sǫh \trs{I am called …}
\cfex{g-yá:s-ǫh \textsc{1s.a}-named-{\stative}}
\ex … akníyasǫh \trs{we two are called …}
\ex … agwáyasǫh \trs{we all are called …}
\ex … agwayasǫ́hǫnyǫh \trs{we all are called …}
\cfex{agwa-yas-ǫ́-h-ǫny-ǫh \textsc{1p.p}-named-{\distributive}-\textsc{euph.h}-{\distributive-\stative} }
\ex … kniyá:sǫh, tniyá:sǫh \trs{we two are called …}
\ex … dwayá:sǫh \trs{we all are called …} (referring to a team name)
\ex … dwayasǫ̱hǫ́:nyǫh \trs{we all are called …} (referring to a list of names)
\ex … syá:sǫh \trs{you (singular) are called …}
\ex … sniyá:sǫh \trs{you two are called …}
\ex … swayá:sǫh \trs{you all are called …} (referring to a team name)
\ex … swayasǫ̱hǫ́:nyǫh \trs{you all are called …} (referring to a list of names)
\ex … gayá:sǫh \trs{it is called …}
\ex … hayá:sǫh \trs{he is called …}
\ex … eyá:sǫh \trs{she is called …}
\ex … gadíyasǫh \trs{they (animals) are called …} (referring to a team name)
\ex … gadiyasǫ́hǫnyǫh \trs{they (animals) are called …} (referring to a list of names)
\ex … hadíyasǫh \trs{they (males) are called …} (referring to a team name)
\ex … hadiyasǫ́hǫnyǫh \trs{they (males) are called …} (referring to a list of names)
\ex … gáeyasǫh \trs{they (females or mixed) are called …} (referring to a team name)
\ex … gaeyasǫ́hǫnyǫh \trs{they (females or mixed) are called …} (referring to a list of names)
\z
\z


\subsection{Asking where someone comes from}
(Use place names in place of the ellipses. For place names, see \sectref{place names}.)

\ea 
\ea 
\gll Gaę́ nǫdahse:?\\
Where you.come.from.someplace\\
\glt \trs{Where do you come from?}
\ex 
\gll … nǫdá:ge:ˀ\\
… where.I.come.from\\
\glt \trs{I come from …}
\z 
\z

\ea 
\ea 
\gll Gaę hǫ́: nǫdi:se:nǫ:?\\
which place where.you.originate.from\\
\glt \trs{Where are you from?}
\ex 
\gll … nǫtgé:nǫ:\\
… where.I.originate.from\\
\glt \trs{I am from …}
\z 
\z 

\ea
\ea 
\gll Gaę hǫ́: disahdęgyǫ:?\\
which place you.come.from.there\\
\glt \trs{Where are you from?}
\ex 
\gll … dwagáhdęgyǫ:\\
… I.come.from.there\\
\glt \trs{I am from …}
\z
\z 

\ea 
\ea 
\gll Gaę hǫ́: tsiˀdrǫˀ?\\
which place you.dwell.there\\
\glt \trs{Where do you live?}
\ex 
\gll … shęh tgiˀdrǫˀ\\
… that I.dwell.there\\
\glt \trs{… is where I live.}
\z 
\z 


\section{Numbers and money} \label{numbers and money}

\subsection{Counting to 10} \label{counting to 10}

\lexicalunitnobracket{géi} \trs{four}
\lexicalunitnobracket{hwíhs} \trs{five}

\end{multicols}

\subsection{Multiples of 10} \label{multiples of 10}
\begin{multicols}{2}
%\pseudoitem{}

\subsection{Multiples of 100} \label{multiples of 100}
\begin{multicols}{2}
%\pseudoitem{}

\CayugaRemark{\cayuga{sgá:t} \trs{one} \stem{de-w-ę́-ˀny-a-we:ˀ} {\dualic}-\textsc{3s.a}-{\semireflexive}-finger-{\joinerA}-verb.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{dekní: dewę́ˀnyawe:ˀ} \trs{200}, \trs{two hundred}
\CayugaRemark{\cayuga{dekní:} \trs{two} \stem{de-w-ę́-ˀny-a-we:ˀ} {\dualic}-\textsc{3s.a}-{\semireflexive}-finger-{\joinerA}-verb.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{some hundreds} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{na̱ˀde-w-ę̱-ˀny-á:-we:ˀ} \exsc{\partitive.\dualic-3s.a}-{\semireflexive}-finger-{\joinerA}-verb.{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{ahsę́ na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{300}, \trs{three hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{géi na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{400}, \trs{four hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{hwíhs na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{500}, \trs{five hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{hyeíˀ na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{600}, \trs{six hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{ja:dáhk na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{700}, \trs{seven hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{degrǫ́ˀ na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{800}, \trs{eight hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{gyo̱hdǫ́: na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{900}, \trs{nine hundreds}
\end{multicols}

\subsection{Multiples of 1000} \label{multiples of 1000}
\begin{multicols}{2}
%\pseudoitem{}
\lexicalunitnobracket{na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{some hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{wa̱hshę́: na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{1000}, \trs{ten hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{dewahshę́: na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{2000}, \trs{twenty hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{[ahsę́ niwahshę́:] na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{3000}, \trs{thirty hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{[géi niwahshę́:] na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{4000}, \trs{fourty hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{[hwíhs niwahshę́:] na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{5000}, \trs{fifty hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{[hyéiˀ niwahshę́:] na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{6000}, \trs{sixty hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{[ja:dáhk niwahshę́:] na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{7000}, \trs{seventy hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{[degrǫ́ˀ niwahshę́:] na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{8000}, \trs{eighty hundreds}
\lexicalunitnobracket{[gyo̱hdǫ́: niwahshę́:] na̱ˀdewę̱ˀnyá:we:ˀ} \trs{9000}, \trs{ninety hundreds}
\end{multicols}

\subsection{Ordinal numbers} \label{ordinal numbers}
Ordinal numbers describe a position in a series of numbers.
\begin{multicols}{2}
%\pseudoitem{}
\lexicalunitnobracket{dawá:gyę:ht} \trs{first} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{d-a-w-á:gyę:ht}  {\cislocative}-{\factual}-\textsc{3s.a}-start.with.{\zeropunctual}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{gyogyę́hdǫh} \trs{first} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{g-yo-gyę́:hd-ǫh} {\cislocative}-\textsc{3s.p}-start.with-{\stative}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{wadǫ́ˀtaˀ} \trs{second} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{w-ad-ǫ́-ˀt-ha} \textsc{3s.a}-{\semireflexive}-resemble-{\causative}-{\habitual}
\lexicalunitnobracket{dekní: wadǫ́ˀtaˀ} \trs{second}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{ahsę́h wadǫ́ˀtaˀ} \trs{third}
\lexicalunitnobracket{hesgá:gǫ:t} \trs{last} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{hes-gá:-gǫ:t} {\translocative}.{\repetitive}-\textsc{3s.a}-persevere. {\stative}}
\end{multicols}

\subsection{Once, twice, three times} \label{number of times}
The following words functioning as “adverbs of time” are thematically related to the ordinal numbers presented in \sectref{ordinal numbers}.
\begin{multicols}{2}
%\pseudoitem{}
\lexicalunitnobracket{hęwá:dra:s} \trs{times} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{h-ę-w-adraˀ-hs} {\cislocative}-{\future}-\textsc{3s.a}-meet-\exsc{\causative.\zeropunctual}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{sgá:t hęwá:dra:s} \trs{once}, \trs{one time}
\lexicalunitnobracket{dekní: hęwá:dra:s} \trs{twice}, \trs{two times}
\lexicalunitnobracket{ahsę́h hęwá:dra:s} \trs{thrice}, \trs{three times}
\end{multicols}

\section{Government and business} \label{Government and Business}
Thematic headwords (in small caps) either provide the English name for a Canadian government organization or constitute a thematic category for grouping related words. For single-word entries such as \textit{haihwahę́:deˀ} (see below), both morpheme breakdowns and translations are provided. In contrast, for compound entries such as \textit{Ǫgwehǫwéh Oihwagéhsǫˀ} (see below), only the approximate translations of each word of the compound are provided.

\begin{multicols}{2}


\thematicheadword{aboriginal affairs}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Ǫgwehǫwéh Oihwagéhsǫˀ}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{Ǫgwehǫ:weh} \trs{First Nations people} 
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{Oihwagehsǫˀ} \trs{affairs, statistics}

\thematicheadword{aboriginal trappers’ federation}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Ǫgwehǫwéh Honęnigyohgwaę́ˀ Hęnadri̱hsdáęhęˀ}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{Ǫgwehǫ:weh} \trs{First Nations people}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{honęnigyohgwaęˀ} \trs{their (males) crowd, group, association}
\pseudoitem{} \cayuga{hęnadri̱hsdaęhęˀ} \trs{they (males) are trappers}

\thematicheadword{aboriginal education council}

\section{High language} \label{ch:High Language}
Gayogo̱ho:nǫˀnéha:ˀ speakers use the term “high language” when referring to words or phrases used in oratory or in Longhouse “doings” (ceremonies). Thematic headwords (in small caps) constitute a thematic category for grouping related words.

\begin{multicols}{2}

\thematicheadword{accomplish a ceremonial task}
\lexicalunitnobracket{ahaihwá:is} \trs{he accomplished or completed a task} (for example, a speech), \trs{he carried out a responsibility} \CayugaRemark{\stem{a-ha-ihwá:-is} {\factual}-\textsc{3s.m.a}-matter-finish.{\punctual}}
\thematicheadword{amulet}
\lexicalunitnobracket{goyáˀdanǫh} \trs{amulet} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{go-yáˀda-nǫh} \textsc{3s.fi.p}-doll-guard.{\stative}}
\thematicheadword{charm society, charm}
\lexicalunitnobracket{otsinǫ̱hgę́ˀdaˀ} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{o-tsinǫ̱hgę́ˀd-aˀ} \textsc{3s.p}-charm-{\nsf}}
\thematicheadword{consensus}
\lexicalunitnobracket{sgaíhwa:t} \trs{one mind} (said when consensus is reached in the High Council), \trs{one idea} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{s-ga-íhw-a:-t} {\repetitive}-\textsc{3s.a}-matter-{\joinerA}-stand.{\stative}}


\columnbreak
\thematicheadword{consider}
\lexicalunitnobracket{ęgátgǫdrahk} \trs{I will sleep on it, consider it} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{ę-g-át-gǫdr-ahk} {\future}-\textsc{1s.a}-{\semireflexive}-pillow-lift.up.{\punctual}}
\thematicheadword{cookhouse}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Ǫtgahdę̱hsdáhkwaˀgeh} \trs{Cookhouse} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{ǫ-t-gah-dę̱hsd-á-hkwa-ˀgeh} \textsc{3s.fi.a}-{\semireflexive}-eye-string.up-{\joinerA}-{\instrumental}-{\on}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{gahsdǫ́hgwage:} \trs{Cookhouse} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{ga-hsdǫ́hgw-age:} \textsc{3s.a}-endure-{\on}}
\lexicalunitnobracket{ganǫhsáǫ:weh} \trs{the cookhouse} (at the Longhouse) 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{ga-nǫhsá-ǫ:weh} \textsc{3s.a}-house-{\typicalizer}}

\columnbreak

\CayugaRemark{\stem{ę-s-at-hah-í:tˀa:-ˀ} {\future}-\textsc{2s.a}-{\semireflexive}-road-take-{\punctual}}

\section{Betting}\label{betting}

Words having to do with the ceremonial betting game.

\begin{multicols}{2}

\lexicalunitnobracket{aˀehędáę:ˀ} \trs{she won the throw} (refers to a betting game and a perfect score, when all the stones come up the same) 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{aˀ-e-hęd-á-ę-:ˀ} {\factual}-\textsc{3s.fi.a}-field-{\joinerA}-lie-{\punctual}}

\lexicalunitnobracket{aˀehędaędǫ́:nyǫ:ˀ} \trs{she won the throw repeatedly, one right after the other} (refers to a betting game and a perfect score, when all the stones come up the same) 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{aˀ-e-hęd-a-ęd-ǫ́:-nyǫ:ˀ} {\factual}-\textsc{3s.fi.a}-field-{\joinerA}-lie.\exsc{euph.d}-{\distributive}-{\distributive.\punctual}}

\lexicalunitnobracket{ahahędáę:ˀ} \trs{he won the throw} (in a betting game) 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{a-ha-hęd-á-ę-:ˀ} {\factual}-\textsc{3s.m.a}-field-{\joinerA}-lie-{\punctual}}

\lexicalunitnobracket{deyǫdetsgęˀihdréhda̱hkwaˀ} 
\CayugaRemark{\stem{de-yǫ-d-e-tsgęˀ-ihdréhd-a̱-hkwaˀ} {\dualic}-\exsc{3s.fi.a-{\semireflexive}}-{\joinerE}-bone-drive-{\joinerA}-{\instrumental}}

\lexicalunitnobracket{ęyǫde̱hsaheˀdanǫ́:na:ˀ} \trs{she will be the bean watcher} (for a betting game)

\lexicalunitnobracket{hahsaheˀdá:gwahs} \trs{he picks out the beans} (for a betting game)

\CayugaRemark{\stem{o-hę́:d-aˀ} \textsc{3s.p}-field-{\nsf}}

\CayugaRemark{\stem{s-hęd-á-ę} \textsc{2s.a}-field-{\joinerA}-lie.{\noaspect}}

\end{multicols}
\section{Ceremonies} \label{ceremonies}
Thematic headwords (in small caps) either provide the English name for a ceremony or constitute a thematic category for grouping related ceremonies and words.

\begin{multicols}{2}
\thematicheadword{All Eaten Up} 
\lexicalunitnobracket{Ga̱hsǫ:ˀ} (restricted ceremony for charms) \CayugaRemark{\stem{ga̱-hsǫ:ˀ} \textsc{3s.a}-eaten.up.{\stative}}
\thematicheadword{Clean Up (ceremony)}
\lexicalunitnobracket{Deyakwetnǫ́hsǫtaˀ} \CayugaRemark{\stem{de-yagwa-t-nǫ́hs-ǫt-haˀ} {\dualic}-\exsc{1p.ex.a}-{\semireflexive}-house-clean.up-{\habitual}}
\thematicheadword{Devil Dance} 
\lexicalunitnobracket{Gaˀnogęyǫ:ˀ} \trs{Grinding the Arrow} (forbidden ceremony) \CayugaRemark{\stem{ga-ˀno-gęyǫ:ˀ} \textsc{3s.a}-arrow-grind.{\stative}}
\thematicheadword{Eagle Feather} 
\lexicalunitnobracket{Ganréˀaˀ} \trs{Eagle Feathers} (For making ceremonial friends. The one who asks for the friendship holds the feathers.) \CayugaRemark{\stem{ga-nréˀ-aˀ} \textsc{3s.a}-eagle.feather-{\nsf}}

\section{Songs} \label{songs}
Miscellaneous terms relevant for medicines.
\begin{multicols}{2}

\CayugaRemark{\stem{a-ha-ęn-á-ganye:-ˀ} {\factual}-\textsc{3s.m.a}-song-{\joinerA}-make.noises-{\punctual}}

\lexicalunitnobracket{ęhswaęnáganye:} \trs{you all will sing}
